<h3><span id="Using_Pick.27s_theorem"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Using_Pick's_theorem"><span class="anchor" id="Using_Pick's_Theorem"></span>Using Pick's theorem</span></h3>
<p>See <a href="/wiki/Pick%27s_theorem" title="Pick's theorem">Pick's theorem</a> for a technique for finding the area of any arbitrary <a href="/wiki/Lattice_graph" title="Lattice graph">lattice polygon</a> (one drawn on a grid with vertically and horizontally adjacent lattice points at equal distances, and with vertices on lattice points).
</p><p>The theorem states:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle T=I+{\tfrac {1}{2}}B-1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>T</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>I</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<mi>B</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle T=I+{\tfrac {1}{2}}B-1}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle T=I+{\tfrac {1}{2}}B-1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9a8bac8f73b426b797951f21ca530c69b21a0ff8" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:16.172ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where <i><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle I}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>I</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle I}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="I" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/535ea7fc4134a31cbe2251d9d3511374bc41be9f" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.172ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span></i> is the number of internal lattice points and <i>B</i> is the number of lattice points lying on the border of the polygon.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_area_formulas">Other area formulas</span></h3>
<p>Numerous other area formulas exist, such as
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle T=r\cdot s,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>T</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>r</mi>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mi>s</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle T=r\cdot s,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="T=r\cdot s," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/aab6ed54484e772842a29d7887a1bf4248da0b09" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:9.2ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Upper_bound_on_the_area">Upper bound on the area</span></h3>
<p>The area <i>T</i> of any triangle with perimeter <i>p</i> satisfies
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle T\leq {\tfrac {p^{2}}{12{\sqrt {3}}}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>T</mi>
<mo>≤<!-- ≤ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<msup>
<mi>p</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mrow>
<mn>12</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mn>3</mn>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle T\leq {\tfrac {p^{2}}{12{\sqrt {3}}}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle T\leq {\tfrac {p^{2}}{12{\sqrt {3}}}},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/300f28cd9dc99b75a3d961630071fbee8194208d" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:10.053ex; height:5.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>with equality holding if and only if the triangle is equilateral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 657">: 657 </span></sup>
</p><p>Other upper bounds on the area <i>T</i> are given by<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p.290">: p.290 </span></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 4{\sqrt {3}}T\leq a^{2}+b^{2}+c^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mn>4</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mn>3</mn>
</msqrt>

<p>both again holding if and only if the triangle is equilateral.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bisecting_the_area">Bisecting the area</span></h3>
<p>There are infinitely many <a href="/wiki/Bisection#Area_bisectors_and_perimeter_bisectors" title="Bisection">lines that bisect the area of a triangle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunn-32">[28]</a></sup> Three of them are the medians, which are the only area bisectors that go through the centroid. Three other area bisectors are parallel to the triangle's sides.
</p><p>Any line through a triangle that splits both the triangle's area and its perimeter in half goes through the triangle's incenter. There can be one, two, or three of these for any given triangle.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_formulas_for_general_Euclidean_triangles">Further formulas for general Euclidean triangles</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_triangle_inequalities" title="List of triangle inequalities">List of triangle inequalities</a></div>
<p>The formulas in this section are true for all Euclidean triangles.
</p>
<h3><span id="Medians.2C_angle_bisectors.2C_perpendicular_side_bisectors.2C_and_altitudes"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Medians,_angle_bisectors,_perpendicular_side_bisectors,_and_altitudes">Medians, angle bisectors, perpendicular side bisectors, and altitudes</span></h3>

</p><p>The altitude from, for example, the side of length <i>a</i> is
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle h_{a}={\frac {2T}{a}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>The product of two sides of a triangle equals the altitude to the third side times the diameter <i>D</i> of the circumcircle:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Altshiller-Court_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Altshiller-Court-33">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p.64">: p.64 </span></sup>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adjacent_triangles">Adjacent triangles</span></h3>
<p>Suppose two adjacent but non-overlapping triangles share the same side of length <i>f</i> and share the same circumcircle, so that the side of length <i>f</i> is a chord of the circumcircle and the triangles have side lengths (<i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, <i>f</i>) and (<i>c</i>, <i>d</i>, <i>f</i>), with the two triangles together forming a <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_quadrilateral" title="Cyclic quadrilateral">cyclic quadrilateral</a> with side lengths in sequence (<i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, <i>c</i>, <i>d</i>). Then<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-36">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 84">: 84 </span></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle f^{2}={\frac {(ac+bd)(ad+bc)}{(ab+cd)}}.\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>

<mi>b</mi>
<mi>d</mi>

<mi>a</mi>

<mrow>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Centroid">Centroid</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Centroid" title="Centroid">Centroid</a></div>
<p>Let <i>G</i> be the centroid of a triangle with vertices <i>A</i>, <i>B</i>, and <i>C</i>, and let <i>P</i> be any interior point. Then the distances between the points are related by<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-36">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 174">: 174 </span></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle (PA)^{2}+(PB)^{2}+(PC)^{2}=(GA)^{2}+(GB)^{2}+(GC)^{2}+3(PG)^{2}.\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>

<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>G</mi>
<mi>A</mi>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mn>2</mn>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (PA)^{2}+(PB)^{2}+(PC)^{2}=(GA)^{2}+(GB)^{2}+(GC)^{2}+3(PG)^{2}.\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="(PA)^{2}+(PB)^{2}+(PC)^{2}=(GA)^{2}+(GB)^{2}+(GC)^{2}+3(PG)^{2}.\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/677e8e9762b14a38b1b55cad61993cad8661e097" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:64.377ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>The sum of the squares of the triangle's sides equals three times the sum of the squared distances of the centroid from the vertices:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle AB^{2}+BC^{2}+CA^{2}=3(GA^{2}+GB^{2}+GC^{2}).}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>

<mi>B</mi>

</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>3</mn>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>G</mi>
<msup>
<mi>A</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>

</msup>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle AB^{2}+BC^{2}+CA^{2}=3(GA^{2}+GB^{2}+GC^{2}).}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="AB^{2}+BC^{2}+CA^{2}=3(GA^{2}+GB^{2}+GC^{2})." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e0813f34f24908b350125c0ce9065bd1155847a3" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:45.767ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[33]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Let <i>q<sub>a</sub></i>, <i>q<sub>b</sub></i>, and <i>q<sub>c</sub></i> be the distances from the centroid to the sides of lengths <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i>. Then<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-36">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 173">: 173 </span></sup>
</p>

<h3><span id="Circumcenter.2C_incenter.2C_and_orthocenter"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Circumcenter,_incenter,_and_orthocenter">Circumcenter, incenter, and orthocenter</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circumcenter" title="Circumcenter">Circumcenter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Incenter" title="Incenter">Incenter</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthocenter" title="Orthocenter">Orthocenter</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carnot%27s_theorem_(inradius,_circumradius)" title="Carnot's theorem (inradius, circumradius)">Carnot's theorem</a> states that the sum of the distances from the circumcenter to the three sides equals the sum of the circumradius and the inradius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Altshiller-Court_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-Altshiller-Court-33">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p.83">: p.83 </span></sup> Here a segment's length is considered to be negative if and only if the segment lies entirely outside the triangle. This method is especially useful for deducing the properties of more abstract forms of triangles, such as the ones induced by <a href="/wiki/Lie_algebra" title="Lie algebra">Lie algebras</a>, that otherwise have the same properties as usual triangles.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_theorem_in_geometry" title="Euler's theorem in geometry">Euler's theorem</a> states that the distance <i>d</i> between the circumcenter and the incenter is given by<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Altshiller-Court_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-Altshiller-Court-33">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p.85">: p.85 </span></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \displaystyle d^{2}=R(R-2r)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>d</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>R</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>R</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>r</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \displaystyle d^{2}=R(R-2r)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\displaystyle d^{2}=R(R-2r)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4b3dc80966816a188b1ec4c8747bdded2b4e6726" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:15.76ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>or equivalently
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {1}{R-d}}+{\frac {1}{R+d}}={\frac {1}{r}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mrow>
<mi>R</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>d</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mrow>
<mi>R</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>d</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mi>r</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {1}{R-d}}+{\frac {1}{R+d}}={\frac {1}{r}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\frac {1}{R-d}}+{\frac {1}{R+d}}={\frac {1}{r}}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e5fcc54cdab2027694aba87f9806229d6bfb686d" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:21.897ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where <i>R</i> is the circumradius and <i>r</i> is the inradius. Thus for all triangles <i>R</i> ≥ 2<i>r</i>, with equality holding for equilateral triangles.
</p><p>If we denote that the orthocenter divides one altitude into segments of lengths <i>u</i> and <i>v</i>, another altitude into segment lengths <i>w</i> and <i>x</i>, and the third altitude into segment lengths <i>y</i> and <i>z</i>, then <i>uv</i> = <i>wx</i> = <i>yz</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Altshiller-Court_33-5"><a href="#cite_note-Altshiller-Court-33">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p.94">: p.94 </span></sup>
</p><p>The distance from a side to the circumcenter equals half the distance from the opposite vertex to the orthocenter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Altshiller-Court_33-6"><a href="#cite_note-Altshiller-Court-33">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p.99">: p.99 </span></sup>
</p><p>The sum of the squares of the distances from the vertices to the orthocenter <i>H</i>  plus the sum of the squares of the sides equals twelve times the square of the circumradius:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Altshiller-Court_33-7"><a href="#cite_note-Altshiller-Court-33">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p.102">: p.102 </span></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle AH^{2}+BH^{2}+CH^{2}+a^{2}+b^{2}+c^{2}=12R^{2}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Angles">Angles</span></h3>
<p>In addition to the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_sines" title="Law of sines">law of sines</a>,  the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_cosines" title="Law of cosines">law of cosines</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_tangents" title="Law of tangents">law of tangents</a>, and the <a href="#Trigonometric_conditions">trigonometric existence conditions</a> given earlier, for any triangle
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a=b\cos C+c\cos B,\quad b=c\cos A+a\cos C,\quad c=a\cos B+b\cos A.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h3><span id="Morley.27s_trisector_theorem"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Morley's_trisector_theorem">Morley's trisector theorem</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Morley%27s_trisector_theorem" title="Morley's trisector theorem">Morley's trisector theorem</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Morley_triangle.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Morley_triangle.svg/220px-Morley_triangle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Morley_triangle.svg/330px-Morley_triangle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Morley_triangle.svg/440px-Morley_triangle.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Morley_triangle.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Morley triangle, resulting from the trisection of each interior angle. This is an example of a <a href="/wiki/Finite_subdivision_rule" title="Finite subdivision rule">finite subdivision rule</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Morley's trisector theorem states that in any triangle, the three points of intersection of the adjacent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angle_trisector" title="Angle trisector">angle trisectors</a> form an equilateral triangle, called the Morley triangle.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Figures_inscribed_in_a_triangle">Figures inscribed in a triangle</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conics">Conics</span></h3>
<p>As discussed above, every triangle has a unique inscribed circle (incircle) that is interior to the triangle and tangent to all three sides.
</p><p>Every triangle has a unique <a href="/wiki/Steiner_inellipse" title="Steiner inellipse">Steiner inellipse</a> which is interior to the triangle and tangent at the midpoints of the sides. <a href="/wiki/Marden%27s_theorem" title="Marden's theorem">Marden's theorem</a> shows how to find the <a href="/wiki/Ellipse#Elements_of_an_ellipse" title="Ellipse">foci of this ellipse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[34]</a></sup> This ellipse has the greatest area of any ellipse tangent to all three sides of the triangle.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mandart_inellipse" title="Mandart inellipse">Mandart inellipse</a> of a triangle is the ellipse inscribed within the triangle tangent to its sides at the contact points of its excircles.
</p><p>For any ellipse inscribed in a triangle <i>ABC</i>, let the foci be <i>P</i> and <i>Q</i>. Then<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {{\overline {PA}}\cdot {\overline {QA}}}{{\overline {CA}}\cdot {\overline {AB}}}}+{\frac {{\overline {PB}}\cdot {\overline {QB}}}{{\overline {AB}}\cdot {\overline {BC}}}}+{\frac {{\overline {PC}}\cdot {\overline {QC}}}{{\overline {BC}}\cdot {\overline {CA}}}}=1.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mrow>

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {{\overline {PA}}\cdot {\overline {QA}}}{{\overline {CA}}\cdot {\overline {AB}}}}+{\frac {{\overline {PB}}\cdot {\overline {QB}}}{{\overline {AB}}\cdot {\overline {BC}}}}+{\frac {{\overline {PC}}\cdot {\overline {QC}}}{{\overline {BC}}\cdot {\overline {CA}}}}=1.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\frac {{\overline {PA}}\cdot {\overline {QA}}}{{\overline {CA}}\cdot {\overline {AB}}}}+{\frac {{\overline {PB}}\cdot {\overline {QB}}}{{\overline {AB}}\cdot {\overline {BC}}}}+{\frac {{\overline {PC}}\cdot {\overline {QC}}}{{\overline {BC}}\cdot {\overline {CA}}}}=1." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3aa55cb2a4b60d1ecae688927cd4f140a5518e91" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:40.258ex; height:7.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Convex_polygon">Convex polygon</span></h3>
<p>Every convex polygon with area <i>T</i> can be inscribed in a triangle of area at most equal to 2<i>T</i>. Equality holds (exclusively) for a <a href="/wiki/Parallelogram" title="Parallelogram">parallelogram</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hexagon">Hexagon</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lemoine_hexagon" title="Lemoine hexagon">Lemoine hexagon</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Hexagon#Cyclic_hexagon" title="Hexagon">cyclic hexagon</a> with vertices given by the six intersections of the sides of a triangle with the three lines that are parallel to the sides and that pass through its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symmedian_point" title="Symmedian point">symmedian point</a>. In either its <a href="/wiki/Polygon#Convexity_and_types_of_non-convexity" title="Polygon">simple form or its self-intersecting form</a>, the Lemoine hexagon is interior to the triangle with two vertices on each side of the triangle.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Squares">Squares</span></h3>
<p>Every acute triangle has three inscribed squares (squares in its interior such that all four of a square's vertices lie on a side of the triangle, so two of them lie on the same side and hence one side of the square coincides with part of a side of the triangle). In a right triangle two of the squares coincide and have a vertex at the triangle's right angle, so a right triangle has only two <i>distinct</i> inscribed squares. An obtuse triangle has only one inscribed square, with a side coinciding with part of the triangle's longest side.  Within a given triangle, a longer common side is associated with a smaller inscribed square. If an inscribed square has side of length <i>q</i><sub><i>a</i></sub> and the triangle has a side of length <i>a</i>, part of which side coincides with a side of the square, then <i>q</i><sub><i>a</i></sub>, <i>a</i>, the altitude <i>h</i><sub><i>a</i></sub> from the side <i>a</i>, and the triangle's area <i>T</i> are related according to<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OS_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-OS-42">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle q_{a}={\frac {2Ta}{a^{2}+2T}}={\frac {ah_{a}}{a+h_{a}}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>q</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>

</semantics>

<p>The largest possible ratio of the area of the inscribed square to the area of the triangle is 1/2, which occurs when <span class="nowrap"><i>a</i><sup>2</sup> = 2<i>T</i></span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>q</i> = <i>a</i>/2</span>, and the altitude of the triangle from the base of length <i>a</i> is equal to <i>a</i>. The smallest possible ratio of the side of one inscribed square to the side of another in the same non-obtuse triangle is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 2{\sqrt {2}}/3=0.94....}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</msqrt>
</mrow>

</mrow>

<mn>0.94....</mn>
</mstyle>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="2{\sqrt {2}}/3=0.94...." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c27b21e3de7dd4c4db125ee8b1f64b132cf6f3ab" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:16.406ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OS_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-OS-42">[38]</a></sup> Both of these extreme cases occur for the isosceles right triangle.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Triangles">Triangles</span></h3>
<p>From an interior point in a reference triangle, the nearest points on the three sides serve as the vertices of the <a href="/wiki/Pedal_triangle" title="Pedal triangle">pedal triangle</a> of that point. If the interior point is the circumcenter of the reference triangle, the vertices of the pedal triangle are the midpoints of the reference triangle's sides, and so the pedal triangle is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Midpoint_triangle" title="Midpoint triangle">midpoint triangle</a> or medial triangle. The midpoint triangle subdivides the reference triangle into four congruent triangles which are similar to the reference triangle.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gergonne_triangle" title="Gergonne triangle">Gergonne triangle</a> or intouch triangle of a reference triangle has its vertices at the three points of tangency of the reference triangle's sides with its incircle. The <a href="/wiki/Extouch_triangle" title="Extouch triangle">extouch triangle</a> of a reference triangle has its vertices at the points of tangency of the reference triangle's excircles with its sides (not extended).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Figures_circumscribed_about_a_triangle">Figures circumscribed about a triangle</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tangential_triangle" title="Tangential triangle">tangential triangle</a> of a reference triangle (other than a right triangle) is the triangle whose sides are on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tangent_line" title="Tangent line">tangent lines</a> to the reference triangle's circumcircle at its vertices.
</p><p>As mentioned above, every triangle has a unique circumcircle, a circle passing through all three vertices, whose center is the intersection of the  perpendicular bisectors of the triangle's sides.
</p><p>Further, every triangle has a unique <a href="/wiki/Steiner_ellipse" title="Steiner ellipse">Steiner circumellipse</a>, which passes through the triangle's vertices and has its center at the triangle's centroid. Of all ellipses going through the triangle's vertices, it has the smallest area.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kiepert_hyperbola" title="Kiepert hyperbola">Kiepert hyperbola</a> is the unique <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conic" title="Conic">conic</a> which passes through the triangle's three vertices, its centroid, and its circumcenter.
</p><p>Of all triangles contained in a given convex polygon, there exists a triangle with maximal area whose vertices are all vertices of the given polygon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specifying_the_location_of_a_point_in_a_triangle">Specifying the location of a point in a triangle</span></h2>
<p>One way to identify locations of points in (or outside) a triangle is to place the triangle in an arbitrary location and orientation in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartesian_plane" title="Cartesian plane">Cartesian plane</a>, and to use Cartesian coordinates. While convenient for many purposes, this approach has the disadvantage of all points' coordinate values being dependent on the arbitrary placement in the plane.
</p><p>Two systems avoid that feature, so that the coordinates of a point are not affected by moving the triangle, rotating it, or reflecting it as in a mirror, any of which give a congruent triangle, or even by rescaling it to give a similar triangle:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Trilinear_coordinates" title="Trilinear coordinates">Trilinear coordinates</a> specify the relative distances of a point from the sides, so that coordinates <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x:y:z}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>:</mo>
<mi>y</mi>
<mo>:</mo>
<mi>z</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x:y:z}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x:y:z" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4fc1d4f62fa3da71416dec491efd083a8c473361" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:7.448ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> indicate that the ratio of the distance of the point from the first side to its distance from the second side is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x:y}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>:</mo>
<mi>y</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x:y}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x:y" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7c856cee7dfad676e081203a32e48077904001dc" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:4.422ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> , etc.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barycentric_coordinates_(mathematics)" title="Barycentric coordinates (mathematics)">Barycentric coordinates</a> of the form <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \alpha :\beta :\gamma }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi>α<!-- α --></mi>
<mo>:</mo>
<mi>β<!-- β --></mi>
<mo>:</mo>
<mi>γ<!-- γ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \alpha :\beta :\gamma }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\alpha :\beta :\gamma " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cdeb88b91752ebc42398e577d5455edc63aab124" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.956ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> specify the point's location by the relative weights that would have to be put on the three vertices in order to balance the otherwise weightless triangle on the given point.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-planar_triangles">Non-planar triangles</span></h2>
<p>A non-planar triangle is a triangle which is not contained in a (flat) plane. Some examples of non-planar triangles in non-Euclidean geometries are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spherical_triangle" title="Spherical triangle">spherical triangles</a> in <a href="/wiki/Spherical_geometry" title="Spherical geometry">spherical geometry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_triangle" title="Hyperbolic triangle">hyperbolic triangles</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_geometry" title="Hyperbolic geometry">hyperbolic geometry</a>.
</p><p>While the measures of the internal angles in planar triangles always sum to 180°, a hyperbolic triangle has measures of angles that sum to less than 180°, and a spherical triangle has measures of angles that sum to more than 180°. A hyperbolic triangle can be obtained by drawing on a negatively curved surface, such as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saddle_surface" title="Saddle surface">saddle surface</a>, and a spherical triangle can be obtained by drawing on a positively curved surface such as a <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">sphere</a>. Thus, if one draws a giant triangle on the surface of the Earth, one will find that the sum of the measures of its angles is greater than 180°; in fact it will be between 180° and 540°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[40]</a></sup> In particular it is possible to draw a triangle on a sphere such that the measure of each of its internal angles is equal to 90°, adding up to a total of 270°.
</p><p>Specifically, on a sphere the sum of the angles of a triangle is
</p>
<dl><dd>180° × (1 + 4<i>f</i>),</dd></dl>
<p>where <i>f</i> is the fraction of the sphere's area which is enclosed by the triangle. For example, suppose that we draw a triangle on the Earth's surface with vertices at the North Pole, at a point on the equator at <a href="/wiki/Prime_meridian" title="Prime meridian">0° longitude</a>, and a point on the equator at <a href="/wiki/90th_meridian_west" title="90th meridian west">90° West longitude</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Great_circle" title="Great circle">great circle</a> line between the latter two points is the equator, and the great circle line between either of those points and the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a> is a line of longitude; so there are right angles at the two points on the equator. Moreover, the angle at the North Pole is also 90° because the other two vertices differ by 90° of longitude. So the sum of the angles in this triangle is <span class="nowrap">90° + 90° + 90° = 270°</span>. The triangle encloses 1/4 of the northern hemisphere (90°/360° as viewed from the North Pole) and therefore 1/8 of the Earth's surface, so in the formula <span class="nowrap"><i>f</i> = 1/8</span>; thus the formula correctly gives the sum of the triangle's angles as 270°.
</p><p>From the above angle sum formula we can also see that the Earth's surface is locally flat: If we draw an arbitrarily small triangle in the neighborhood of one point on the Earth's surface, the fraction <i>f</i> of the Earth's surface which is enclosed by the triangle will be arbitrarily close to zero. In this case the angle sum formula simplifies to 180°, which we know is what Euclidean geometry tells us for triangles on a flat surface.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Triangles_in_construction">Triangles in construction</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Truss" title="Truss">Truss</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edificio_Fuller_(Flatiron)_en_2010_desde_el_Empire_State_crop_boxin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2818" data-file-width="1489" decoding="async" height="416" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Edificio_Fuller_%28Flatiron%29_en_2010_desde_el_Empire_State_crop_boxin.jpg/220px-Edificio_Fuller_%28Flatiron%29_en_2010_desde_el_Empire_State_crop_boxin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Edificio_Fuller_%28Flatiron%29_en_2010_desde_el_Empire_State_crop_boxin.jpg/330px-Edificio_Fuller_%28Flatiron%29_en_2010_desde_el_Empire_State_crop_boxin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Edificio_Fuller_%28Flatiron%29_en_2010_desde_el_Empire_State_crop_boxin.jpg/440px-Edificio_Fuller_%28Flatiron%29_en_2010_desde_el_Empire_State_crop_boxin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edificio_Fuller_(Flatiron)_en_2010_desde_el_Empire_State_crop_boxin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Flatiron_Building" title="Flatiron Building">Flatiron Building</a> in New York is shaped like a <a href="/wiki/Triangular_prism" title="Triangular prism">triangular prism</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rectangle" title="Rectangle">Rectangles</a> have been the most popular and common geometric form for buildings since the shape is easy to stack and organize; as a standard, it is easy to design furniture and fixtures to fit inside rectangularly shaped buildings. But triangles, while more difficult to use conceptually, provide a great deal of strength. As computer technology helps <a href="/wiki/Architect" title="Architect">architects</a> design creative new buildings, triangular shapes are becoming increasingly prevalent as parts of buildings and as the primary shape for some types of skyscrapers as well as building materials. In Tokyo in 1989, architects had wondered whether it was possible to build a 500-story tower to provide affordable office space for this densely packed city, but with the danger to buildings from <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquakes</a>, architects considered that a triangular shape would be necessary if such a building were to be built.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twsMarE34_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-twsMarE34-45">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Broadway_(Manhattan)" title="Broadway (Manhattan)">Broadway</a> crisscrosses major avenues, the resulting blocks are cut like triangles, and buildings have been built on these shapes; one such building is the triangularly shaped <a href="/wiki/Flatiron_Building" title="Flatiron Building">Flatiron Building</a> which real estate people admit has a "warren of awkward spaces that do not easily accommodate modern office furniture" but that has not prevented the structure from becoming a landmark icon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twsMarE32_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-twsMarE32-46">[42]</a></sup> Designers have made houses in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> using triangular themes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twsMarE35_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-twsMarE35-47">[43]</a></sup> Triangle shapes have appeared in churches<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twsMarE36_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-twsMarE36-48">[44]</a></sup> as well as public buildings including colleges<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twsMarE37_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-twsMarE37-49">[45]</a></sup> as well as supports for innovative home designs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twsMarE41_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-twsMarE41-50">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Triangles are sturdy; while a rectangle can collapse into a <a href="/wiki/Parallelogram" title="Parallelogram">parallelogram</a> from pressure to one of its points, triangles have a natural strength which supports structures against lateral pressures. A triangle will not change shape unless its sides are bent or extended or broken or if its joints break; in essence, each of the three sides supports the other two. A rectangle, in contrast, is more dependent on the strength of its joints in a structural sense. Some innovative designers have proposed making <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bricks" title="Bricks">bricks</a> not out of rectangles, but with triangular shapes which can be combined in three dimensions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twsMarE33_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-twsMarE33-51">[47]</a></sup> It is likely that triangles will be used increasingly in new ways as architecture increases in complexity. It is important to remember that triangles are strong in terms of rigidity, but while packed in a <a href="/wiki/Tessellation" title="Tessellation">tessellating</a> arrangement triangles are not as strong as <a href="/wiki/Hexagon" title="Hexagon">hexagons</a> under compression (hence the prevalence of hexagonal forms in <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a>). Tessellated triangles still maintain superior strength for <a href="/wiki/Cantilever" title="Cantilever">cantilevering</a> however, and this is the basis for one of the strongest man made structures, the <a href="/wiki/Space_frame" title="Space frame">tetrahedral truss</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Euclid defines isosceles triangles based on the number of equal sides, i.e. <i>only two equal sides</i>. An alternative approach defines isosceles triangles based on shared properties, i.e. <i>equilateral triangles are a special case of isosceles triangles</i>. <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Isosceles_triangle" title="wikt:Isosceles triangle">wikt:Isosceles triangle</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i>n</i> external angles of any <i>n</i>-sided <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/convex" title="wikt:convex">convex</a> polygon add up to 360 degrees.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Again, in all cases "mirror images" are also similar.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">All pairs of congruent triangles are also similar; but not all pairs of similar triangles are congruent.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

</li>
